    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="812df03a502742b6adf08d0a8a39ebaf">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f9c97c16-e287-4f06-83aa-deabe37724ef'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d174183674" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries are applied to cold-resistant energy storage hindered by phase transitions and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> migration of traditional carbonate-based electrolytes at low temperatures. The desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> is a crucial step in impeding the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>, which primarily attributes to the robust solvent coordination of Na<sup>+</sup>. Herein, a low-temperature adaptive electrolyte with an ultraweakly coordinated 1,3-dioxolane (DOL) is designed for constructing anion-rich solvation structure in a diglyme (G2)-based electrolyte. The electronegativity of the oxygen atoms of G2 is attenuated by dipole-dipole interaction between DOL and G2. As the temperature drops, the weakened Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2) interaction leads to increased anionic coordination and less solvent coordination, facilitating the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup>. This anionic-enhanced solvation structure contributes to the formation of stable solid electrolyte interface at the hard carbon (HC) anode, which accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> transport and diminishing the voltage polarization at low temperatures. Consequently, the HC anode can retain a high capacity of 203.9 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> (1 C) at ‒50&nbsp;°C, and the pouch cell composed of HC||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at ‒30&nbsp;°C achieves a capacity retention of 92.43% after 100 cycles at 0.1 C. This strategy guides the design of ultra-low temperature electrolytes and broadens the range of applications for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405363-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Attributed to the small Stokes radius and low solvation energy of sodium ions in most organic solvents and the significant advantages of sodium resources,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0001_R_d174183551e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold great potential in large-scale energy storage and the electrification of transportation in extremely low-temperature environments (especially ≤ ‒30&nbsp;°C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0002_R_d174183551e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Energy Storage">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, extraordinary efforts have been devoted to the development of new electrode materials for SIBs at room temperatures (RT).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0003_R_d174183551e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. eScience Small Methods">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon (HC) has emerged as the most promising anode material for commercialization due to its excellent structural stability, cost-effectiveness, and outstanding reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0004_R_d174183551e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ChemSusChem Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the voltage polarization on the HC surface at low temperatures (LT) increases dramatically, and results in a serious drop in battery capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0005_R_d174183551e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is typically due to the slow migration of Na<sup>+</sup> at LT caused by the high melting points of traditional carbonate-based electrolytes. This makes it challenging for SIBs to operate under low-temperature conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0006_R_d174183551e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - eScience Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the design of low-temperature electrolytes (≤ ‒30&nbsp;°C) is pivotal to improving the performance of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Using linear-ethers with lower melting point has been reported as a practical method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0007_R_d174183551e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A. Joule Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the freezing point of the electrolyte has been reduced and the ion conductivity has been improved. The linear ether solvent represented by G2 is prone to coordinating with Na<sup>+</sup> to form a stable pentagonal chelation structure. The first solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>, dominated by strong coordinating solvents, makes Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation as the decisive step more adverse at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0008_R_d174183551e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To accelerate the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup>, constructing solvation structures with anion-rich electrolyte is an effective approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0009_R_d174183551e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-concentration electrolytes (HCEs) feature anion-rich solvation structures but suffer from high cost, high viscosity and high weight ratio, which limit their application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0010_R_d174183551e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mate. Futures Nat. Commun. ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Local high-concentration electrolytes (LHCEs) effectively overcome the high viscosity barrier of HCEs. However, the high cost and environmental threats of fluorinated diluents pose serious challenges in practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0011_R_d174183551e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Energy Lett. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett. Nat. Energy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To avoid the utilization of fluorinated diluents and ensure a solvation structure with rich anions, a weak solvation electrolyte (WSE) is a promising emerging system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0012_R_d174183551e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. ACS Energy Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to the superfluous anion-dominated aggregate configurations (AGG) in electrolytes, the electrolyte has a higher melting point and lower ion conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0013_R_d174183551e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Mater. Devices Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Small">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This limits its application at ultra-low temperatures. Evidently, low ionic conductivity or high desolvation energy at LT will slow down Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Thereby this increases the voltage polarization on the HC surface and causes the cut-off potential of HC to reach earlier during the cycle, resulting in severe capacity fading of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0014_R_d174183551e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a temperature-adaptive solvation structure is crucial for balancing the high ionic conductivity and low desolvation energy barrier of the electrolytes. Nonetheless, the research about the sensitivity of solvation structure of electrolytes at continuously decreasing operating temperatures is still in a nascent state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0015_R_d174183551e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we construct a low-temperature adaptive electrolyte with a low salt concentration. Specifically, 1,3-dioxolane (DOL) as an ultraweak solvent is mixed into 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> diglyme (G2) electrolyte. This electrolyte can form an anion-enhanced and solvent-weakened solvation structure at LT, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0001">1</a>. The dipole-dipole interaction between DOL and G2 reduces the electronegativity of the oxygen in G2 and weakening the ion-dipole interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2). Thus, as the temperature drops from room temperature to low temperature, this leads to more anions and less G2 molecules involved in primary solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>. This accelerates transport of Na<sup>+</sup> and induces the formation of uniform and continuous solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on HC at LT. This results in lower voltage polarization on the surface of HC and leveraging the plateau capacity of HC. This strikingly facilitates HC-based SIBs to have higher capacity and more stable cycling performance at LT.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7158a778-a9ab-4373-b06e-6a70f5dd0eec/aenm202405363-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7158a778-a9ab-4373-b06e-6a70f5dd0eec/aenm202405363-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/192bf06e-c608-4003-b88b-8d445256b3a8/aenm202405363-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7158a778-a9ab-4373-b06e-6a70f5dd0eec/aenm202405363-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405363-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405363-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Solvation Structure at RT and Physical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>Primarily, Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (DOL) has a lower binding energy compared to Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2), as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2a</a>. Additionally, Na<sup>+</sup> can form a stable five-membered chelation structure with the negative-charged multidentate oxygen of G2 molecules. This endows the G2-based electrolyte high ionic conductivity, but regretfully, impedes Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation. DOL is a low polarity and low melting point (‒95&nbsp;°C) cyclic ether that exhibits dipole-dipole interactions with G2. Explicitly, <sup>1</sup>H and <sup>17</sup>O nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) were studied in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). <sup>1</sup>H (G2) displays an upfield chemical shift in G2/DOL than G2, while the chemical shift of the <sup>17</sup>O (G2) shifts downfield. This implies that the dipole-dipole interactions between G2 and DOL weaken the shielding effect of electron clouds of O (G2) on atomic nucleus, indicating a decrease in the electron cloud density of O (G2). This demonstrates that DOL attenuates the electronegativity of donor O (G2), thereby diminishing the coordination of G2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0016_R_d174183551e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Sci. ACS Nano">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, DOL was blended into 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 with a volume ratio of 3:1 to prepare 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL for the low-temperature adaptive electrolyte. 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 as the reference electrolyte was compared and analyzed. First, the FTIR at 25&nbsp;°C was tested to demonstrate the coordination between Na<sup>+</sup> and oxygen atoms in the solvents in the electrolytes, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, compared to pure DOL, the characteristic peaks of DOL in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL are basically no change, indicating that DOL has an ultraweak solvation ability and hardly participates in the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup>. Raman spectroscopy shows the shift of the stretching vibration peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup> to estimate the coordination between anions and Na<sup>+</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2c</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0017_R_d174183551e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, the peak of anions in 2.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 slightly shifts positively, indicating that the decreased mole amount of G2 will not have a considerable impact on the coordination of anions. However, the peak of anions in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL significantly shifts positively compared to G2 based electrolytes. Additionally, the chemical shift of <sup>23</sup>Na in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL shifts from ‒7.128&nbsp;ppm to ‒7.747&nbsp;ppm compared to 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2d</a>. This is mainly attributed to the dipole-dipole interactions between DOL and G2, which weaken the coordination of O (G2), resulting in the solvation structure enhanced by anions in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL, which is synergistic for the evolution of an inorganic-rich SEI.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a78453ac-7b31-4882-a44e-88dc995336f7/aenm202405363-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a78453ac-7b31-4882-a44e-88dc995336f7/aenm202405363-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1b6c7cf-a22a-4fec-a261-81152a928f00/aenm202405363-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a78453ac-7b31-4882-a44e-88dc995336f7/aenm202405363-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405363-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405363" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup>‒G2 and Na<sup>+</sup>‒DOL. b) <sup>1</sup>H NMR of G2, DOL and G2/DOL (1:3, vol.). c) Raman spectra of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, 2.0&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. d) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. e,f) RDF of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations obtained from MD simulations for electrolytes at 25&nbsp;°C. g) Coordination number (CN) of different components in two electrolytes at 25&nbsp;°C. h) DSC curves of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. i) viscosity from 20&nbsp;°C to ‒50&nbsp;°C, and j) ionic conductivity at the decreasing temperatures range from 20&nbsp;°C to ‒50&nbsp;°C of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. <i>T<sub>m</sub></i>: melting point temperature; <i>T<sub>c</sub></i>: cold crystallization temperature.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To quantitatively elucidate the solvation structure of the two electrolytes, classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed at 25&nbsp;°C. The radial distribution function (RDF) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2e,f</a> displays the distribution of each component around Na<sup>+</sup> in two electrolytes at 25&nbsp;°C. The average coordination number (CN) values of Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2) in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL are 5.979 and 4.733, respectively. The CN of Na‒F (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>) increases from 0.233 to 1.092, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2g</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). This indeed indicates that DOL weakens the coordination oxygen activity of G2, thereby reducing the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2) and increasing the coordination of anions.</p>
            
            <p>The phase transition temperatures of the two electrolytes were evaluated by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) and cryo-polarizing microscope. Remarkably, the melting point of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is ‒91&nbsp;°C, while the melting point of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 is only ‒46&nbsp;°C, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2h</a>. Moreover, 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 generates small ice crystals at ‒50&nbsp;°C and emerges a large-scale phase transition at ‒70&nbsp;°C. 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is a pure liquid at ‒50&nbsp;°C and solvent crystallization does not occur until it is cooled to ‒110&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Variable temperature viscosity tests were conducted on these two electrolytes in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2i</a>. Unlike the viscosity of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, which rises dramatically with decreasing temperatures, the viscosity of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL bestows a slightly growth trend. Simultaneously, the ionic conductivity (IC) of the electrolytes was calculated based on the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements at different temperatures, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0002">2j</a>. Apparently, 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL maintains a higher ionic conductivity which is less affected by temperature. Especially, the ionic conductivity of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is 0.49 and 3.39 mS cm<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒50&nbsp;°C, respectively. This is mainly attributed to the lower viscosity of electrolytes, which results in higher ionic conductivity at LT (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0018_R_d174183551e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405363-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Low-Temperature Adaptive Solvation Structure of Electrolytes</h3>
            
            <p>It is pivotal to investigate the solvation structure of electrolytes under low-temperature conditions to analyze the operation mechanism of batteries at LT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0019_R_d174183551e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature Energy Storage Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, MD was conducted on both electrolytes to illustrate the coordination of solvents and anions around Na<sup>+</sup> at ‒25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C, as displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3a–c</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2, S3</a> (Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3a–c</a> represents the RDF and CN of two electrolytes at ‒50&nbsp;°C. Compared with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, the CN of G2 in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL decreased from 5.723 to 4.243 at ‒50&nbsp;°C. Concurrently, the CN of anions at ‒50&nbsp;°C increased from 0.735 (0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2) to 1.579 (0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL). This indicates that the anion-rich solvation structure still exists in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at LT. Additionally, to recognize the temperature dependence on the solvation structures of the electrolytes, The interpolations of the CN of G2 and anions around Na<sup>+</sup> at ‒50 and 25&nbsp;°C were compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3d</a>. The CN of Na‒O (G2) in both electrolytes at ‒50&nbsp;°C decreased compared to CN at 25&nbsp;°C, with Δ<i>CN</i> of ‒0.49 for 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL and ‒0.256 for 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, respectively. The CN of G2 decreases more significantly with temperature drops from 25&nbsp;°C to ‒50&nbsp;°C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL, mainly due to the ion-dipole interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2). Concretely, DOL weakens the electronegativity of donor oxygen of G2, making G2 more effortless to detach from the first solvation shell. Unlike that, the CN of Na‒F (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>) was incremental similarly, increasing by 0.487 in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL and 0.502 in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2. Generally, as the temperature declines in both electrolytes, G2 coordination decreases and anionic coordination increases. However, due to the weakening of the solvation ability of G2 by DOL, reduction of G2 coordination is more pronounced in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e3f3f7e5-7267-4868-8010-8e65091612f6/aenm202405363-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e3f3f7e5-7267-4868-8010-8e65091612f6/aenm202405363-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7341adf2-e7a9-4109-86d5-ad0449a16114/aenm202405363-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e3f3f7e5-7267-4868-8010-8e65091612f6/aenm202405363-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405363-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405363" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) RDF of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations obtained from MD simulations in two electrolytes at ‒50&nbsp;°C. c) Coordination number (CN) of different components in two electrolytes at ‒50&nbsp;°C. d) The difference in coordination number (<i>ΔCN</i>) of different components in two electrolytes at ‒50&nbsp;°C compared to 25&nbsp;°C. e,f) Raman spectra and 1H NMR of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25, ‒25, and ‒50&nbsp;°C. g,h) Nyquist plots of Na||Na batteries in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL from 20&nbsp;°C to ‒50&nbsp;°C. i) Activation energy of desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> derived from Nyquist plots.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Raman spectra and <sup>1</sup>H NMR at different temperatures demonstrated the changes in the solvation structure of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL with decreasing temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3e,f</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6, S7</a>, Supporting Information). The lower temperature had a positive shift in the characteristic peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup> in two electrolytes, indicating the increasing of anionic coordination. However, the <sup>1</sup>H NMR showed that the difference in chemical shifts of H (G2) in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL and G2/DOL gradually diminishes with decreasing temperature, and H (DOL) also shows a similar trend. Conversely, there is slightly change in the chemical shift difference of H in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2. It follows that the solvent coordination decreases evidently and the anion coordination increases in the 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL as the temperature drops from 25&nbsp;°C to ‒50&nbsp;°C. This means that 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL not only has a solvation structure with more anions at 25&nbsp;°C, but also exhibits a low-temperature adaptability.</p>
            
            <p>The migration of Na<sup>+</sup> from electrolyte to electrodes (anode and cathode) during the battery operation mainly includes four steps: Na<sup>+</sup> conduction in the electrolyte, desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup> passing through SEI, and Na<sup>+</sup> solid-state diffusion of electrodes. Among them, the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> is considered as the determining step, which depends on the primary solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0020_R_d174183551e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To evaluate the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> energy (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i>), EIS at different temperatures was tested in Na||Na symmetric batteries in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3g,h</a>. The activation energy can be calculated using the classical Arrhenius equation in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5, S6</a> (Supporting Information). The energy of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is 14.33&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>‒1</sup>, while the energy of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 are 31.67 and 31.85&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0003">3i</a>. This demonstrates that 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL has a lower desolvation energy, which is attributed to the decrease in G2 coordination. This temperature sensitivity of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL makes for speedy desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> at LT.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405363-sec-0050-title">2.3 Interface Chemistry and Electrochemical Polarization of HC</h3>
            
            <p>The solvation structure of electrolytes is closely related to the electrolyte-electrode interface, which affects the cycling stability of the battery. To reveal the reactivity of two electrolytes with different solvation configurations at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C, the energies of highest occupied and lowest unoccupied molecular orbitals (HOMO and LUMO) were calculated by density functional theory (DFT). The Na[G2]<sub>1.6</sub>[DOL]<sub>0.7</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>]<sub>1.1</sub> at 25&nbsp;°C and the Na[G2]<sub>1.4</sub>[DOL][PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>]<sub>1.6</sub> at ‒50&nbsp;°C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is higher compared with the Na[G2]<sub>2</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>]<sub>0.2</sub> at 25&nbsp;°C and the Na[G2]<sub>1.9</sub>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>‒</sup>]<sub>0.7</sub> at ‒50&nbsp;°C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, respectively, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0004">4a</a>. Lower LUMO energy indicates enhanced electron acceptance of the configuration, which scarcely results in preferential reduction at the anode interface. This demonstrates that the solvation structure enhanced by anions in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL can effectively mitigate solvent decomposition and facilitate the induction of anion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0021_R_d174183551e1028" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Noteworthy, this is conductive to obtaining a robust SEI enriched with inorganic components, which can alleviate the continuous electrolyte consumption and promote long-term stable operation of SIBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d165d651-0a82-47bd-af7d-49a44aff5002/aenm202405363-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d165d651-0a82-47bd-af7d-49a44aff5002/aenm202405363-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e339ce7-f0f9-40e6-819c-edd28d18cfac/aenm202405363-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d165d651-0a82-47bd-af7d-49a44aff5002/aenm202405363-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405363-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405363" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Solvation structures of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C and their corresponding HOMO and LUMO. Schematic diagrams of solvation configuration near the anode interface and HRTEM images of HC anode after cycling in b) 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, c) 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at ‒50&nbsp;°C, and d–f) their respective XPS spectra of C 1s, O 1s and F 1s.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Compared with the solvent-dominated coordination of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, the anion-enhanced coordination of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL prioritizes anion deposition near the HC anode interface, as shown in the schematic diagram in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0004">4b,c</a>. The SEI formed on the HC anode with two electrolytes after cycling at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C was intuitively captured using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0004">4b,c</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9, S10</a> (Supporting Information). The SEI on the HC anode with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is notably uniform and continuous with an average thickness of 1.03&nbsp;nm at ‒50&nbsp;°C. However, the SEI with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 is inhomogeneous and discontinuous with a thickness ranging from 0.65&nbsp;nm to 2.84&nbsp;nm at ‒50&nbsp;°C. Additionally, compared to 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL maintains a thinner and more uniform SEI at 25&nbsp;°C. This is mainly attributed to the weakened solvent coordination and enhanced anion coordination in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL, which leads to the preferential decomposition of anions in the solvated clusters near the anode interface.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was applied to characterize the composition of SEI. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0004">4d–f</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11, S12</a> (Supporting Information) show the atomic percentages and photoelectron spectra images of C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s for SEI formed in two electrolytes at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C. The C 1s spectra displays four characteristic peaks, corresponding to CF<sub>3</sub> (293.6&nbsp;eV), C = O (290.6&nbsp;eV), C‒O (287.1&nbsp;eV), and C‒C (284.6&nbsp;eV), respectively. Three characteristic peaks are deconvoluted in the O 1s spectra, corresponding to Na‒Auger (538.8&nbsp;eV), C = O (533.7&nbsp;eV), and Na<sub>2</sub>O (532.5&nbsp;eV), respectively. The deconvoluted peaks in the F 1s spectra is ascribed to C‒F (688.6&nbsp;eV) and NaF (684.6&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0022_R_d174183551e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These characteristic peaks essentially derive from the organic components generated by solvent decomposition and the inorganic components generated by anion decomposition in the SEI of the HC anode. Strikingly, the SEI in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL contains more inorganic components rich in NaF, which has good electronic insulation and fast ion transport. This can effectively block further decomposition of the electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0023_R_d174183551e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Energy Lett. ACS Energy Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the temperature-adaptive solvation structure of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is conducive to the formation of thin and uniformly dense SEI.</p>
            
            <p>The reversible electroplating/stripping electrochemistry of sodium metal can evaluate the low-temperature performance of electrolytes. The Na||Cu cell in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL exhibits better cycling stability and an average coulombic efficiency value of 98.59% over 100 cycles at ‒40&nbsp;°C in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a,b</a> (Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the Na||Na symmetric cell in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL exhibits a smoother voltage fluctuation, with an overpotential of only 1&nbsp;mV for 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>‒2</sup> at ‒40&nbsp;°C, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c</a> (Supporting Information). This indicates that G2/DOL based electrolytes paired with sodium metal anode have high reversible electroplating/stripping electrochemistry at low temperatures. Besides, understanding the sodium storage mechanism of HC is a prerequisite for improving its low-temperature performance. Specifically, the capacity of HC in ether electrolytes is mainly divided into two parts, including the slope region where Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption occurs in carbon pores and defects, and the plateau region where Na<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent insertion occurs between carbon layers and Na<sup>+</sup> filling in carbon closed pores, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5a</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0024_R_d174183551e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - eScience Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To identify the determining step that limits the kinetic properties of HC, the Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) of Na||HC with two electrolytes was performed at ‒40&nbsp;°C in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). The low potential plateau region of HC exhibits a smaller diffusion coefficient in both electrolytes, indicating that this is the decisive step in the kinetic migration of HC. In spite of that, compared with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, there is speedier ion diffusion in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. Overall, the plateau capacity of HC is more susceptible to the influence of ion diffusion and migration. This leads to an considerably increased voltage polarization at high current density and low temperatures, thereby advancing the cut-off voltage and making it difficult to fully utilize the HC capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405363-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405363-bib-0025_R_d174183551e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Energy Carbon">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cd690b1b-21d9-4bb0-bab6-3793dd422343/aenm202405363-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cd690b1b-21d9-4bb0-bab6-3793dd422343/aenm202405363-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1eba83fd-0483-4c7b-8299-85b39505bc8b/aenm202405363-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cd690b1b-21d9-4bb0-bab6-3793dd422343/aenm202405363-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405363-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405363" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustrations of the mechanisms for Na<sup>+</sup> storage in HC. b) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) testing of Na||HC cells with two electrolytes at 25&nbsp;°C.c) The polarizations, which are the difference between the peak potentials corresponding to A and A' in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). d) Rate capability in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25&nbsp;°C. e) Charge/discharge curves of HC at different rates in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25&nbsp;°C. f) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) testing of Na||HC cells with two electrolytes at ‒40&nbsp;°C. g) The polarizations, which are the difference between the peak potentials corresponding to A and A' in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). h) Discharge curves, i) slope capacity and plateau capacity of HC in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 1 C (280&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup>) at ‒20, ‒30, ‒40 and ‒50&nbsp;°C. j) cycle stability of Na||HC with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at ‒40&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To demonstrate the voltage polarization of HC under high current density in two electrolytes, the variable scan rates CV at 0.5, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0 and 2.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> were tested at 25&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15, S16</a> (Supporting Information). The redox peaks of A and A' correspond to the entry and exit of Na<sup>+</sup> in HC at low potential, respectively. The difference between the peak positions is the polarization of the battery. Obviously, the polarization of HC is smaller in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5c</a>. This is chiefly attributed to the temperature-adaptive solvation structure in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL, which alleviates the voltage polarization of HC by facilitating the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>. The HC anode exhibits superior rate capability in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5d,e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), which has the highest reversible capacity of 228.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5 C,196.5 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 10 C and 143.1 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 15 C, compared to that of 219.0 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5 C, 136.0 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 10 C and 72.1 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 15 C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, respectively.</p>
            
            <p>The CV of Na||HC at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> also indicates that HC has smaller polarization in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at ‒40&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5f</a>. The polarizations of Na||HC in two electrolytes obtained from the variable scanning rates CV in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) indicate that HC still has lower polarization in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL with different current rates at ‒40&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5g</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5h</a> compares the 1 C (280&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup>) discharge capacity of HC in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at ‒20, ‒30, ‒40 and ‒50&nbsp;°C. This demonstrates that HC can achieve more capacity at LT in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL, which retains 243.9 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒20&nbsp;°C, 237.2 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒30&nbsp;°C, 223.9 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒40&nbsp;°C and 206.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒50&nbsp;°C, respectively.The discharge curve of HC is divided into a slope region (1.5–0.1&nbsp;V) and a plateau region (0.1–0.01&nbsp;V), and statistically analyze their capacities at different temperatures in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5i</a>. The capacity of the HC slope region decreased gradually in both electrolytes, mainly due to the slow diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> with decreasing temperatures. HC has higher slope capacity retention in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. Compared with the sudden drop in plateau capacity of HC from 160.73 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 20&nbsp;°C to 25.58 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒50&nbsp;°C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, the plateau capacity of HC in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL decreases from 162.83 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 20&nbsp;°C to 156.41 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒50&nbsp;°C. Typically, this is on account of faster ion diffusion of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL paired with HC, resulting in smaller voltage polarization at high current densities and low temperatures. This allows HC to achieve higher capacity at low temperatures and high rates.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the cycling performance of the HC anode at 25, ‒40, and ‒50&nbsp;°C was tested in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0005">5j</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). Compared with 78.9% of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, the capacity retention rate of HC after 1000 cycles at 5 C in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL is as high as 88.5% at 25&nbsp;°C, indicating that HC has better stability with high current densities in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. Besides, HC exhibits excellent low-temperature performance in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. After 900 cycles at ‒40&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention rate is 91.9%. The capacity retention rates after 200 cycles at 1 C at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒50&nbsp;°C are 94.48% and 97.25%, respectively. This superior performance is attributed to the low-temperature adaptive solvation structure. Enhanced anion coordination and weakened solvent coordination facilitate to the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> on the HC and the formation of rich-inorganic SEI, resulting in more desirable than the currently reported low-temperature electrolytes for SIBs, as listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405363-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performance of Full Cell</h3>
            
            <p>We also have tested the low-temperature performance of 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL in the cathode material of SIBs. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was selected as the cathode with Na metal anode to assemble a coin cell. Rate performance of Na||NVP coupled with both electrolytes were evaluated. NVP paired with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL achieves a capacity of up to 74.7 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 10 C, while it exclusively remains 60.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 10 C with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2, as demonstrated in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22–S24</a> (Supporting Information). Surprisingly, it can operate normally at LT, and specifically preserve a capacity of 79.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at ‒40&nbsp;°C, which is equivalent to 80.0% of the capacity at 25&nbsp;°C in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). This indicates that temperature-adaptive electrolytes are not only compatible with NVP, but can also upgrade the rate performance and low-temperature performance of NVP cathode.</p>
            
            <p>To further verify the feasibility of electrolytes in SIBs, the coin full cell with NVP cathode and pre-cycled HC anode was assembled and subjected to rate performance testing at 25&nbsp;°C. It displays that the full cell coupled with 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL can still retain a capacity of 71.6 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 10 C, while the cell using 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 can only bestow a capacity of 52.3 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0006">6a</a>. Additionally, the performance of the full cell was evaluated at ‒40&nbsp;°C and it was manifested that the full cell using 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL can preserve 64.8% of its capacity at 25&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0006">6b</a>. After 50 cycles of 0.1 C (10&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup>) at 25&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention rate was 95.7%, and then after 50 cycles of 0.1 C at ‒40&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention rate was 92.8%, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0006">6c</a>. To test the practical application of electrolytes, a piece pouch cell (45 mAh) was assembled for cycling testing in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0006">6d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information). After cycling 100 cycles at 25&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention rate was 86.7%. Then, the pouch cell was cycled 100 cycles at ‒30&nbsp;°C, and the capacity retention rate was 92.43% in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405363-fig-0006">6e</a>. Therefore, 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL electrolyte with a temperature-adaptive solvent structure holds great potential in the practical application of SIBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405363-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a41f4f41-f8aa-44f0-a053-7bfc394dd7fb/aenm202405363-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a41f4f41-f8aa-44f0-a053-7bfc394dd7fb/aenm202405363-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a40eb0a-7fb2-47d0-ab8c-7ab321499b12/aenm202405363-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a41f4f41-f8aa-44f0-a053-7bfc394dd7fb/aenm202405363-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405363-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405363" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate capability of full cells in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2 and 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25&nbsp;°C. (b-c) Charge/discharge curves and cycle stability of the full cell in 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒40&nbsp;°C. (d) The schematic diagram of the piece pouch cell using 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL. (e) Cycle stability of the pouch cell at 25&nbsp;°C and ‒30&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Additionally, the temperature-adapted solvation structure may widespreadly exist in G2/DOL derivative electrolytes. We have tested the low-temperature performance of G2/THF, DME/DOL and DME/THF based electrolytes, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). HC can still retain 242.5 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, 243.9 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> and 247.7 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> in G2/THF, DME/DOL and DME/THF based electrolytes at ‒40&nbsp;°C, with capacity retention rates of 95.71%, 95.41% and 95.44% after 200 cycles, respectively. This is mainly attributed to the anion enhanced solvation structure in G2/DOL derivative electrolytes, which facilitates the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and accelerates ionic transport at low temperatures. The reason for the ubiquitous temperature adaptability in G2/DOL derivative electrolytes is mainly attribute to the similarity of solvent molecular structures, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information).The derivative solvents of G2 include monoglyme (DME), triglyme (G3), and tetraglyme (G4). Due to the presence of ‒O‒C‒C‒O‒ groups in G2 and its derivatives, they are prone to form varying numbers of five membered chelating structures with Na<sup>+</sup>. The derivative solvents of DOL are cyclic ethers such as THF and MeTHF. The cyclic ethers are all five membered rings complexed with Na<sup>+</sup> monodentate. Monodentate chelating cyclic ethers have weak solvation ability, and mix them with G2 and its derivative based electrolytes is beneficial for constructing anion enhanced solvation structures at low temperatures. This accelerates the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby promoting the low-temperature performance of SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405363-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405363-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we present a low-temperature adaptive electrolyte that is compatible with high Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation rates for SIBs by regulating solvation structure. Due to the weakened ion-dipole interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>‒O (G2), 0.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> G2/DOL has a low-temperature responsive solvation structure. As the temperature decreases, solvent coordination reduces and anion coordination increases in the first solvated shell of Na<sup>+</sup>. The temperature-sensitive solvation structure accelerates the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup>, mitigating the voltage polarization on the HC surface. This enables HC to achieve higher capacity retention at high current density and low temperatures, thereby enhancing the low-temperature performance of SIBs. The HC anode realized a high capacity of 220.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> (1 C) at ‒40&nbsp;°C, with 91.9% capacity retention after 900 cycles. At ‒30&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention rate of HC||NVP pouch cell after 100 cycles at 0.1 C is 92.43%, indicating that electrolytes with low-temperature adaptation can effectively improve the low-temperature performance of SIBs. Noteworthy, temperature-adapted solvation structures universally exist in G2/DOL derivative electrolytes, which have exceedingly outstanding low-temperature performance. This provides a new way for designing low-temperature sodium-ion batteries and broadens their application for energy storage in extremely cold regions.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
